As a unique water-stable cathode, Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNM) usually becomes vulnerable to water molecules after element substitution treatment to suppress the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering arrangement, which causes limited Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. Herein, we show that these issues can be addressed simultaneously by rational designing the transition-metal (TM) layer to achieve both water-stable and Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy disordering structures. Density functional theory calculations reveal that the water-stability of the layered oxide cathode can be correlated to the surface adsorption energy of H<sub>2</sub>O molecules. In the TM layer, the Co/Mn and Fe/Mn units exhibit a much lower adsorption energy than that of the Li/Mn unit, and hence the H<sub>2</sub>O molecule prefers to be absorbed on Co/Mn and Fe/Mn units rather than Li/Mn. Moreover, the Li/Mn unit in the TM layer can suppress the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering structure in NNM to improve the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. As a consequence, the well-designed Na<sub>2/3</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>5/18</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode can not only maintain its original crystal structure and electrochemical property after water soaking treatment but also exhibit high rate capability (78% capacity retention at 20 C) and excellent cycling stability (87% capacity retention after 1000 cycles).</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D15220%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYi%2BZhang%252C%2BMiaomiao%2BWu%252C%2BWei%2BTeng%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D13%26contentID%3Dacsami.0c00386%26title%3DWater-Stable%2BCathode%2Bfor%2BHigh%2BRate%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D15227%26publicationDate%3DApril%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright Â© 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290834&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Defects in solids"> Defects in solids </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292200&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Layers"> Layers </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291754&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Molecules"> Molecules </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292316&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transition metals"> Transition metals </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode">cathode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Transition+Metal+Oxides&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_transition%20metal%20oxides">transition metal oxides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na%2B%2Fvacancy+Disordering&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na%2B%2Fvacancy%20disordering">Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy disordering</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Water-stable&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_water-stable">water-stable</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20battery">sodium ion battery</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Layered transition-metal (TM) oxides have been widely used as cathode materials for rechargeable batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1â4)</a> LiTMO<sub>2</sub> cathodes, such as LiCoO<sub>2</sub> and LiNi<sub>0.8</sub>Co<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, have been commercialized as high energy density cathodes in lithium-ion batteries and widely used in portable electronic devices and electric vehicles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> However, the low geographic distribution of the Li resource and high production cost limit its application for large-scale energy storage. Considering the abundant resource of sodium (2.3% of sodium and 0.0017% of lithium in the Earthâs crust) and the similar chemical storage mechanism, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref7" aria-label="reference 4 and 7">(4,7)</a> sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been investigated systematically as ideal alternatives for grid storage applications, while sodium-layered TM oxides (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>) (TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, or Cu) are considered as a suitable Na<sup>+</sup> host. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 8 to 22">(8â22)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Based on the atomic environment of Na sites and the number of TM slabs with different arrangements of O layers, most of the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> can be classified into P2 and O3 phases. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Theoretically, Na ions in the prismatic site have higher ionic mobility because the migration of Na ions from one prismatic site to another requires only one transit of the rectangular face, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> hence the P2 phase is beneficial for being used as a high rate cathode. However, most of P2 phase cathodes, such as Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>VO<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>TeO<sub>6</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> and Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> suffer from the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering structure. The Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> is relevant to the arrangement of cations in the TM layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> As a typical example, the Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode exhibits a <span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer inline-formula-styles" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/medium/am0c00386_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i3"></span> superlattice Ni/Mn ordering structure (Ni<sup>2+</sup> are surrounded by six Mn<sup>4+</sup>) in the TM layer, which induces the formation of a Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering arrangement in the Na layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> This Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering structure usually causes a higher Na migration energy barrier and step-like voltage plateau during insertion/extraction processes, leading to rapid capacity loss and poor rate capability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref28 ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 25 and 28 to 30">(25,28â30)</a> Partially replacing the Ni<sup>2+</sup> or Mn<sup>4+</sup> of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> by Li<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Al<sup>3+</sup>, Ti<sup>4+</sup>, Co<sup>3+</sup>, or Fe<sup>3+</sup> can disturb the Ni/Mn ordering arrangement in the TM layer so that the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering arrangement can be suppressed. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 31 to 38">(31â38)</a> Although this strategy can effectively improve the electrochemical performance, the Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> would become vulnerable to the moisture environment after element substitution by Co<sup>3+</sup> or Fe<sup>3+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 32 and 39 to 44">(32,39â44)</a> During the charge/discharge process, the water molecules can react with the electrolyte or release proton to dissolve the cathode materials, resulting in low cycle performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 44 to 48">(44â48)</a> A hypothesis was proposed that the water molecules could easily be inserted into the interlayer spacing of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> once the Ni/Mn ordering in the TM layer was destroyed by element substitution. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Unfortunately, till now the water-insertion mechanism is unclear.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, the water-stability of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> after substitution of Li<sup>+</sup>, Co<sup>3+</sup>, or Fe<sup>3+</sup> for Ni<sup>2+</sup> have been investigated and we found that the water-stability of these cathodes can be correlated to the surface adsorption energy of H<sub>2</sub>O. Density functional theory (DFT) calculation results revealed that the surface adsorption energy value of Co/Mn or Fe/Mn units are far less than that of Li/Mn and Ni/Mn units, hence the H<sub>2</sub>O molecule prefers to be absorbed on the Co/Mn and Fe/Mn units. After water-soaking treatment for 24 h, the Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2/3</sub>Co<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes are easily invaded by H<sub>2</sub>O molecules and exhibit poor cycling stability, while the Na<sub>2/3</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>5/18</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode can maintain its original crystal structure as well as electrochemical performance, demonstrating a higher water-stability. Moreover, the partial substitution of Li<sup>+</sup> for Ni<sup>2+</sup> can eliminate the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering in the Na layer, leading to a solid solution behavior with superior Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics during the insertion/extraction processes. Our findings provide an effective strategy for the achievement of a water-stable cathode with high rate capability for SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Characterization of the Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p">P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Co<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>5/18</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (hereafter denoted as NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM, respectively) samples were prepared <i>via</i> a solâgel method followed by heat treatment at 900 Â°C. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a,b, the crystal structure of NNM is built on oxygen ion framework with Na ions and TM layers, and the Ni/Mn cations in the TM layer form an <span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer inline-formula-styles" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/medium/am0c00386_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></span> superlattice (honeycomb) ordering arrangement. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> In the Na layers, there exist two distinct sites: one is the edge-sharing site between TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra (namely Na<sub><i>e</i></sub>) and the other is the face-sharing site between TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra (namely Na<sub><i>f</i></sub>). These Na ions are orderly distributed in the Na layer, which is induced by the Ni/Mn cation ordering arrangement. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref32" aria-label="reference 15 and 32">(15,32)</a> All the Na ions occupy the âprismaticâ sites in Na ion layers and the oxygen ion framework are stacked in a âABBAABâ mode.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/medium/am0c00386_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/large/am0c00386_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a,b) Crystal structure of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with ABBAAB layer stacking mode. Two distinct prismatic sites in the Na ion layer and Ni/Mn cationic ordering in the TM layer. (câf) XRD and Rietveld plots of NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM samples. (g) Comparison of XRD patterns for NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM samples. Inset shows the zoom between 26 and 30Â°.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/large/am0c00386_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c00386&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>câf, the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM samples reveal that all the samples are well crystalized and maintain the original hexagonal structure with a <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> space group, confirming the formation of solid solution after partial substitution of Co<sup>3+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>, or Li<sup>+</sup> for Ni<sup>2+</sup> in the NNM compound. Detailed Rietveld refinements are performed to calculate the lattice parameters, and the refined crystallographic data are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/suppl_file/am0c00386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g, two obvious superstructure peaks can be observed at 27.3 and 28.5Â° from the XRD patterns of the NNM sample (insert picture), which are caused by the cation ordering arrangement in the NNM crystal structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref32 ref39 ref50 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref32 ref39 ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 15, 32, 39, 50 and 51">(15,32,39,50,51)</a> These two superstructure peaks disappear in the XRD patterns of NFNM and NLNM samples, demonstrating that the cations ordering in the TM layer can be suppressed by the substitution of Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Li<sup>+</sup> for Ni<sup>2+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref32 ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref32 ref52" aria-label="reference 27, 32 and 52">(27,32,52)</a> However, these two superstructure peaks still exist in the NCNM sample, indicating that the cation ordering arrangement still maintained after the substitution of Ni<sup>2+</sup> by 1/9 Co<sup>3+</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM samples show the 10â100 Î¼m secondary particles composed of plate-stacked primary particles with a hexagonal morphology and roughly 1â5 Î¼m (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>aâd). Energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mappings (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/suppl_file/am0c00386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>) indicate the homogeneous distribution of Na, Mn, Ni, and O elements in the NNM sample. The high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>eâh exhibit the TM layer of all samples. The 100 plane can be clearly observed with an interplanar distance of 0.25 nm. In addition, the interplanar distance of the adjacent lattice fringes are about 0.56 nm for all samples, which can be assigned to the 002 plane (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>iâl). Based on the Rietveld results, the refined crystal structures of NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM samples show interslab distances of about 5.57, 5.58, 5.59, and 5.56 Ã (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>mâp), respectively, which is in accordance with the interslab distance of the 002 plane from HRTEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>iâl). These results indicating that the substitution of Co, Fe, or Li in the NNM compound has very little effect on the interlayer structure.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/medium/am0c00386_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/large/am0c00386_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (aâd) SEM images of NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM samples. (eâh) HRTEM images of the 002 plane in NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM samples. (iâl) HRTEM images of the 100 plane in NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM samples. (mâp) The refined crystal structures and the corresponding interlayer distance of NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/large/am0c00386_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c00386&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Water-Stability</h3><div class="NLM_p">According to previous studies, the P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> usually becomes vulnerable to the atmosphere environment after element substitution. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref53" aria-label="reference 32, 39 to 44 and 53">(32,39â44,53)</a> To investigate the water-stability, all samples are soaked in deionized water for 24 h, then dried at 60 Â°C overnight (denoted as W-NNM, W-NCNM, W-NFNM, and W-NLNM), as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/suppl_file/am0c00386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Compared with NNM and NLNM samples, the XRD patterns of W-NNM and W-NLNM show almost no changes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). In contrast, two new peaks around 13 and 27Â° (denoted as 002â² and 004â²) are formed in W-NCNM and W-NFNM samples, which are caused by H<sub>2</sub>O insertion into the interlayer spacing of NCNM and NFNM. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref42" aria-label="reference 39 and 42">(39,42)</a> These results confirm that the Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes become vulnerable to water molecules after the substitution of Co<sup>3+</sup> or Fe<sup>3+</sup> for Ni<sup>2+</sup>. As an example, the insertion of H<sub>2</sub>O destroys the layer structure of the NFNM sample (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/suppl_file/am0c00386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/medium/am0c00386_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/large/am0c00386_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD patterns of NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM samples before/after water soaking treatment. (b) Charge/discharge curves and (c) cycling stability of NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM samples before/after water soaking treatment. (d) DFT calculated Î<i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> value for Ni/Mn, Co/Mn, Fe/MN, and Li/Mn units.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/large/am0c00386_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c00386&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b compares the charge/discharge curves of NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM electrodes before/after water soaking treatment. For NNM and NLNM electrodes, the charge/discharge profiles show almost no change after water soaking treatment. In contrast, both NCNM and NFNM electrodes show a noticeable difference in charge/discharge profiles. The discharge capacity of the W-NCNM electrode decreased from 85.4 to 77.2 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> while that of the W-NFNM electrode decreased from 86.1 to 72.6 mA h g<sup>â1</sup>. After 1000 charge/discharge cycles at 5 C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c), the capacity retention of NCNM and NFNM electrodes exhibit visible decay before/after water soaking: NCNM electrode keeps 78% (61.8 mA h g<sup>â1</sup>) of its initial capacity, while the retention of the W-NCNM electrode is 64% (50.5 mA h g<sup>â1</sup>); the NFNM electrode maintains 86% (69.8 mA h g<sup>â1</sup>) of its initial capacity, while the retention of the W-NFNM electrode is 63% (46.5 mA h g<sup>â1</sup>). These results prove that the substitution of Co or Fe for Ni in the NNM cathode lead to an unstable structure against water, while the Li-substitution exhibits high water stability. In addition, the doping amount of Li shows no effect to the water-stability of the NLNM cathode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/suppl_file/am0c00386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">Guided by DFT calculations, we consider that the water stability of the layered cathode is correlated to the surface adsorption energy of the H<sub>2</sub>O molecule (denoted as Î<i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>). For the NNM cathode, the TM layer consists of numerous Ni/Mn units. After the substitution of Co<sup>3+</sup> or Fe<sup>3+</sup> for Ni<sup>2+</sup>, new units of Co/Mn and Fe/Mn are formed in the TM layer of NCNM and NFNM cathodes, respectively. In order to verify whether the Li<sup>+</sup> exists in the TM layer after the substitution of Li for Ni, <sup>7</sup>Li solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) is carried out to further investigate the local environments of Li<sup>+</sup> in the NLNM sample. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/suppl_file/am0c00386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, the resonance at 850 ppm is assigned to Li in an octahedrally distorted site within the Na layer while the resonances around 1840 and 2200 ppm can be assigned to Li<sup>+</sup> in the the octahedral site within the TM layer, demonstrating that the Li/Mn units are formed in the TM layer of the NLNM cathode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> The adsorption energies of H<sub>2</sub>O on these considered units are defined as<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/medium/am0c00386_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where, <i>E</i><sub>slab+H<sub>2</sub>O</sub> means the total energy of slab and H<sub>2</sub>O, <i>E</i><sub>slab</sub> denotes the slab energy and <i>E</i><sub>H<sub>2</sub>O</sub> represents the energy of H<sub>2</sub>O.</div><div class="NLM_p last"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d shows the Ni/Mn, Co/Mn, Fe/Mn, and Li/Mn unit structure and the corresponding adsorption energies of a H<sub>2</sub>O molecule. The most stable adsorption form of the H<sub>2</sub>O molecule corresponds to the lowest value of Î<i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 55 and 56">(55,56)</a> The calculated Î<i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> for Ni/Mn unit is found to be a positive value of 0.47 eV, indicating that the interaction between the Ni/Mn unit and H<sub>2</sub>O molecule is virtually impossible. For the Li/Mn unit, the H<sub>2</sub>O molecule is weakly attracted to the Li site because the value of Î<i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> is only â0.1 eV. In contrast, the H<sub>2</sub>O molecule prefers to absorb on the Co/Mn and Fe/Mn units because the calculated Î<i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> of Co/Mn and Fe/Mn units show much lower values of â1.06 and â1.37 eV, respectively. Therefore, the H<sub>2</sub>O molecules on Co/Mn or Fe/Mn units have more opportunities to penetrate into the interlayer spacing than that of the Li/Mn unit. These calculated results can explain the formation of 002â² and 004â² new peaks in the XRD patterns of NCNM and NFNM samples after the water soaking treatment (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Sodium Storage Performance of NLNM</h3><div class="NLM_p">Although both NNM and NLNM electrodes are quite stable under water conditions, the substitution of Li<sup>+</sup> may affect the electrochemical behavior. To study the Na ion mobility kinetics in NNM and NLNM electrodes, Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients are extracted by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), according to the following equation <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/medium/am0c00386_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>here, Ï is the time of galvanostatic injection step, <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> is the weight of active material, <i>V</i><sub>m</sub> is the molar volume, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> is the molar weight, Î<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> is the value change between the neighboring charge open circuit potential, and Î<i>E</i><sub>Ï</sub> is the potential change during the galvanostatic injection step.</div><div class="NLM_p">Compared to the voltage variation of the NNM electrode, the equilibrium voltage of NLNM shows a continuous change, suggesting the elimination of the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering structure in the Na layer (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of NNM and NLNM electrodes are calculated based on the GITT curves, and the variation as a function of the Na<sup>+</sup> concentration is plotted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b. The diffusion coefficient of the NLNM electrode slightly decreases from 10<sup>â9</sup> to 10<sup>â10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>â1</sup>, while that of the NNM electrode has a dramatic fluctuation between 10<sup>â10</sup> and 10<sup>â12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>â1</sup> in the Na<sup>+</sup> content range of 1/3 â¤ <i>z</i> â¤ 2/3. Such results clearly manifest that the cation disordering structure in the TM layer can prevent the ordering arrangement of Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy in the Na layer, favoring rapid Na<sup>+</sup> mobility during the charge/discharge process. In addition, the capacitive contribution of NNM, NCNM, NFNM, and NLNM electrodes are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/suppl_file/am0c00386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, which confirms a capacitive sodium storage mechanism of the NLNM electrode.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/medium/am0c00386_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/large/am0c00386_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Voltageâtime profiles obtained using GITT recorded during the second charge for NNM and NLNM samples. (b) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients in NNM and NLNM electrodes. (c) <i>Ex situ</i> XRD patterns of NLNM electrodes collected during the second charge/discharge process at different potentials of 2.4, 3.2, 3.5, 3.8, and 4.1 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/large/am0c00386_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c00386&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><i>Ex situ</i> XRD tests are carried out to elucidate the structure evolution of cation disordering NLNM samples during the charge/discharge process. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, during the sodium extraction process, the peaks of 002 and 004 continuously move toward lower angles, indicative of the expansion in the <i>c</i> axis. This is because the removal of Na ions increases the electrostatic repulsion between the neighboring oxygen layers. Meanwhile, the 100 and 102 peaks gradually move to higher angles, corresponding to the contraction of the TMâTM plane because the oxidation of TM leads to smaller ionic radius. During the sodium insertion process, all these peaks can be back to the original position, demonstrating the outstanding reversibility NLNM electrodes. Compared with their pristine XRD patterns, the P2 structures are well maintained without any phase transition, which agrees with the GITT results and confirms the solid-solution behavior. The 12.5 and 25.5Â° in the XRD patterns at 4.1 V belong to the hydrated phase based on previous references. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref39" aria-label="reference 30 and 39">(30,39)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, the charge/discharge profiles of NNM obviously involve two staircase-type plateaus at â¼3.3 and 3.6 V, indicating the existence of two different Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering arrangements. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref27 ref32 ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref27 ref32 ref58" aria-label="reference 25, 27, 32 and 58">(25,27,32,58)</a> These two voltage plateaus vanish and become sloping lines in cation-disordered NLNM electrode, demonstrating the suppression of Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering after the substitution of Li<sup>+</sup> for Ni<sup>2+</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b). As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, the NNM electrode presents sharp redox peaks at â¼3.3 and 3.6 V in the corresponding cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves. These curves become smoother and the peaks disappear after Li<sup>+</sup> substitution, which is in accordance with the charge/discharge profiles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). In addition, the charge/discharge profiles, CV curves, and rate performance of NCNM and NFNM samples are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/suppl_file/am0c00386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/medium/am0c00386_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/large/am0c00386_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a,b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles, (c,d) CV curves, (e) rate performance, and (f) cycling stability of NNM and NLNM electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/asset/images/large/am0c00386_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c00386&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e, the NNM and NLNM cathodes deliver capacities of 87.2 and 92.5 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> at 0.5 C, respectively. When increasing the current density to 20 C, the NLNM electrode still achieves a high retention of 78% (72.2 mA h g<sup>â1</sup>), while only about 35% (30.5 mA h g<sup>â1</sup>) is retained for the NNM electrode. These results prove the advantages of suppressing Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering structure for high rate capability. Such a trend is also observed for NNM and NLNM electrodes cycled at 5 C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f). After 1000 cycles, the NLNM electrode retain nearly 87% (76.5 mA h g<sup>â1</sup>) of the initial discharge capacity, while the NNM electrode shows only 62% (43.9 mA h g<sup>â1</sup>) capacity retention, further confirming that the suppression of Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering favors long cycling stability. Therefore, the substitution of Li<sup>+</sup> for Ni<sup>2+</sup> in Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> can turn Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering into a disordering structure, which modifies the insertion mechanism from phase transition to solid solution behavior, leading to the improved electrochemical performance for sodium storage.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we found that the water-stability of the layered oxide cathode is correlated to the surface adsorption energy of H<sub>2</sub>O molecules, which can be influenced by adjusting the element components in the TM layer. Based on DFT calculation, the Î<i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> value of Co/Mn or Fe/Mn units are far less than that of Li/Mn units, hence the H<sub>2</sub>O molecule prefers to be absorbed on the Co/Mn and Fe/Mn units rather than the Li/Mn unit. As a result, the NCNM and NFNM are vulnerable to water conditions, while the NLNM is stable against water. The NLNM can retain its original crystal structure and outstanding cycle stability even after water soaking treatment for 24 h. Moreover, the NLNM electrode exhibits excellent rate capability (78% retention at 20 C) because the substitution of Li<sup>+</sup> for Ni<sup>2+</sup> eliminates the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering arrangement in NNM. Our present work provides a simple strategy for the rational design of water-stable oxide cathode materials with high rate capability for practical application.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> Synthesis of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p last">Stoichiometric NaAc, NiAc<sub>2</sub> and MnAc<sub>2</sub> (Ac = CH<sub>3</sub>COO<sup>â</sup>) were dissolved in 40 mL of deionized water to form a clear solution. NaAc (5% excess) was added to compensate the sodium loss at high temperature. The solution was totally evaporated at 80 Â°C to obtain a green precursor. The precursor was first sintered in air at 350 Â°C for 4 h and then at 900 Â°C for another 12 h with a heating rate of 2 Â°C min<sup>â1</sup>. The sample was quenched to room temperature and then transferred to the drying room. For water-sensitivity test, the sample was first exposed to air for 24 h and then soaked in deionized water for 24 h, followed by drying at 60 Â°C overnight.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14"> Synthesis of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Co<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p last">Stoichiometric NaAc, CoAc<sub>3</sub>, NiAc<sub>2</sub>, and MnAc<sub>2</sub> were dissolved in 40 mL of deionized water to form a clear solution. NaAc (5% excess) was added to compensate the sodium loss at high temperature. The solution was totally evaporated at 80 Â°C to obtain a powder precursor. The precursor was first sintered in air at 350 Â°C for 4 h and then at 900 Â°C for another 12 h with a heating rate of 2 Â°C min<sup>â1</sup>. All samples were quenched to room temperature and then transferred to the drying room. For water-sensitivity test, the sample was first exposed to air for 24 h and then soaked in deionized water for 24 h, followed by drying at 60 Â°C overnight.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i15"> Synthesis of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p last">Stoichiometric NaAc, Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, NiAc<sub>2</sub>, and MnAc<sub>2</sub> were dissolved in 40 mL of deionized water to form a clear solution. NaAc (5% excess) was added to compensate the sodium loss at high temperature. The solution was totally evaporated at 80 Â°C to obtain a powder precursor. The precursor was first sintered in air at 350 Â°C for 4 h and then at 900 Â°C for another 12 h with a heating rate of 2 Â°C min<sup>â1</sup>. The sample was quenched to room temperature and then transferred to the drying room. For water-sensitivity test, the sample was first exposed to air for 24 h and then soaked in deionized water for 24 h, followed by drying at 60 Â°C overnight.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i16" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i16"> Synthesis of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>5/18</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p last">Stoichiometric NaAc, LiAc, NiAc<sub>2</sub>, and MnAc<sub>2</sub> were dissolved in 40 mL of deionized water to form a clear solution. NaAc (5% excess) was added to compensate the sodium loss at high temperature. The solution was totally evaporated at 80 Â°C to obtain a powder precursor. The precursor was first sintered in air at 350 Â°C for 4 h and then at 900 Â°C for another 12 h with a heating rate of 2 Â°C min<sup>â1</sup>. The sample was quenched to room temperature and then transferred to the drying room. For water-sensitivity test, the sample was first exposed to air for 24 h and then soaked in deionized water for 24 h, followed by drying at 60 Â°C overnight.</div></div><div id="sec4_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i17" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i17"> Electrochemical Measurements for SIB</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical test of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2/3</sub>Co<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>2/3</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>5/18</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes were carried out using CR2032 coin-type cells with sodium disc as the anode and glass fiber (whatman GF/D) as the separator. For the preparation of cathode electrodes, a homogeneous slurry was first obtained by mixing a 80 wt % active material, 10 wt % Ketjen black (EC600JD) and 10 wt % poly(vinyl difluoride) (PVDF) in <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidinone, and then the slurry was pasted uniformly onto an aluminum foil current collector and dried in vacuum at 95 Â°C for 12 h. The loading mass of the active material was 2.5â3 mg cm<sup>â2</sup>. The electrolyte was a mixture solution of ethylene carbonate and dimethyl carbonate 1:1 (w/w) containing 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> and 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate additives. The cell was assembled in a glove box filled with dried argon gas (H<sub>2</sub>O, O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 0.1 ppm).</div><div class="NLM_p last">CV and GITT were performed on a Bio-Logic VMP3 multichannel electrochemical workstation. The charge/discharge measurements were carried out on a Neware measurement system at 25 Â°C. The CV was scanned between 2.4 and 4.1 V at rates of 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 mV s<sup>â1</sup>. Charge/discharge profiles were attained at various current densities in the voltage range of 2.4â4.1 V. For GITT test, the cells were charged at 0.1 C for 30 min, followed by a relaxation of 10 h under open circuit.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i18">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c00386?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c00386</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">EDS, HRTEM images, <sup>7</sup>Li solid-state NMR spectrum, and other characterization data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/suppl_file/am0c00386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/suppl_file/am0c00386_si_001.pdf">am0c00386_si_001.pdf (1.53 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.0c00386" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy d. is the main property of rechargeable batteries that has driven the entire technol. forward in past decades.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Effects of transition-metal mixing on na ordering and kinetics in layered P2 oxides</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zheng, Chen; Radhakrishnan, Balachandran; Chu, Iek-Heng; Wang, Zhenbin; Ong, Shyue Ping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review Applied</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered P2 oxides are promising cathode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries.  In this work, we systematically investigate the effects of transition-metal (TM) mixing on Na ordering and kinetics in the NaxCo1-yMnyO2 model system using d.-functional-theory (DFT) calcns.  The DFT-predicted 0-K stability diagrams indicate that Co-Mn mixing reduces the energetic differences between Na orderings, which may account for the redn. of the no. of phase transformations obsd. during the cycling of mixed-TM P2 layered oxides compared to a single TM.  Using ab initio mol.-dynamics simulations and nudged elastic-band calcns., we show that the TM compn. at the Na(1) (face-sharing) site has a strong influence on the Na site energies, which in turn impacts the kinetics of Na diffusion towards the end of the charge.  By employing a site-percolation model, we establish theor. upper and lower bounds for TM concns. based on their effect on Na(1) site energies, providing a framework to rationally tune mixed-TM compns. for optimal Na diffusion.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.3389/fchem.2019.00335"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Air-Stable Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> Cathodes for Sodium Storage</span>. <i>Front.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Air-stable NaxTMO2 cathodes for sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Yi; Zhang, Renyuan; Huang, Yunhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Frontiers in Chemistry (Lausanne, Switzerland)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Frontiers Media S.A.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries are considered to be the most promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale stationary energy storage applications due to the abundant sodium resource in the Earth' crust and as a result, relatively low cost.  Sodium layered transition metal oxides (NaxTMO2) are proper Na-ion cathode materials because of low cost and high theor. capacity.  Currently most researchers focus on the improvement of electrochem. performance such as high rate capability and long cycling stability.  However, for NaxTMO2, the structure stability against humid atm. is essentially important since most of them are instable in air, which is not favorable for practical application.  Here we provide a comprehensive review of recent progresses on air-stable NaxTMO2 oxides.  Several effective strategies are discussed, and further investigations on the air-stable cathodes are prospected.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201400083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hasa, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Scrosati, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hassoun, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Performance Na<sub>0.5</sub>[Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.13</sub>Mn<sub>0.63</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report a facile method to topotactically synthesize Na1.25V3O8 nanowires with a novel hierarchical zigzag structure.  The unique morphol. can provide an increased electrode-electrolyte contact area and better strain accommodation; also the topotactic intercalation method can improve structure integrity and robustness.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NaCrO2 cathode for high-rate sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yu, Chan-Yeop; Park, Jae-Sang; Jung, Hun-Gi; Chung, Kyung-Yoon; Aurbach, Doron; Sun, Yang-Kook; Myung, Seung-Taek</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries offer a potential alternative or complementary system to lithium-ion batteries, which are widely used in many applications.  For this purpose, layered O3-type NaCrO2 for use as a cathode material in sodium-ion batteries was synthesized via an emulsion-drying method.  The produced NaCrO2 was modified using pitch as a carbon source and the products were tested in half and full cells using a NaPF6-based non-aq. electrolyte soln.  The carbon-coated NaCrO2 cathode material exhibits excellent capacity retention with superior rate capability up to a rate of 150 C (99 mA h (g oxide)-1), which corresponds to full discharge during 27 s.  The surface conducting carbon layer plays a critically important role in the excellent performance of this cathode material.  We confirmed the reaction process with sodium using X-ray diffraction and X-ray absorption spectroscopy.  Thermal anal. using time-resolved X-ray diffraction also demonstrated the structural stability of carbon-coated Na0.5CrO2.  Due to the excellent performance of the cathode material described herein, this study has the potential to promote the accelerated development of sodium-ion batteries for a large no. of applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms7954"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Most P2-type layered oxides exhibit Na+/vacancy-ordered superstructures because of strong Na+-Na+ interaction in the alkali metal layer and charge ordering in the transition metal layer.  These superstructures evidenced by voltage plateaus in the electrochem. curves limit the Na+ ion transport kinetics and cycle performance in rechargeable batteries.  Here we show that such Na+/vacancy ordering can be avoided by choosing the transition metal ions with similar ionic radii and different redox potentials, for example, Cr3+ and Ti4+.  The designed P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 is completely Na+/vacancy-disordered at any sodium content and displays excellent rate capability and long cycle life.  A sym. sodium-ion battery using the same P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 electrode delivers 75% of the initial capacity at 12C rate.  Our contribution demonstrates that the approach of preventing Na+/vacancy ordering by breaking charge ordering in the transition metal layer opens a simple way to design disordered electrode materials with high power d. and long cycle life.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3478"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guignard, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Didier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Darriet, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bordet, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">ElkaÃ¯m, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delmas, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">P2-Na<sub>x</sub>VO<sub>2</sub> System as Electrodes for Batteries and Electron-Correlated Materials</span>. <i>Nat. Here the authors present the detailed phase diagram of the layered P2-NaxVO2 system detd. from electrochem. intercalation/deintercalation in Na batteries and in situ x-ray diffraction expts.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An Abnormal 3.7 Volt O3-Type Sodium-Ion Battery Cathode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Peng-Fei; Xin, Hanshen; Zuo, Tong-Tong; Li, Qinghao; Yang, Xinan; Yin, Ya-Xia; Gao, Xike; Yu, Xiqian; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">57</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered O3-type sodium oxides (NaMO2, M=transition metal) commonly exhibit an O3-P3 phase transition, which occurs at a low redox voltage of â¼3 V (vs. Na+/Na) during sodium extn. and insertion, with the result that almost 50 % of their total capacity lies at this low voltage region, and they possess insufficient energy d. as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (NIBs).  Therefore, development of high-voltage O3-type cathodes remains challenging because it is difficult to raise the phase-transition voltage by reasonable structure modulation.  A new example of O3-type sodium insertion materials is presented for use in NIBs.  The designed O3-type Na0.7Ni0.35Sn0.65O2 material displays a highest redox potential of 3.7 V (vs. Na+/Na) among the reported O3-type materials based on the Ni2+/Ni3+ couple, by virtue of its increased Ni-O bond ionicity through reduced orbital overlap between transition metals and oxygen within the MO2 slabs.  This study provides an orbital-level understanding of the operating potentials of the nominal redox couples for O3-NaMO2 cathodes.  The strategy described could be used to tailor electrodes for improved performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.7b05176"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, H.-R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">OuYang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stavitski, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wan, L.-J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Designing Air-Stable O3-Type Cathode Materials by Combined Structure Modulation for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. Herein, a combined structure modulation is proposed to tackle concurrently the two handicaps via reducing Na layers spacing and simultaneously increasing valence state of transition metals.  Guided by d. functional theory calcns., we demonstrate such a modulation can be subtly realized through Co-substitution of one kind of heteroatom with comparable electronegativity and another one with substantially different Fermi level, by adjusting the structure of NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 via Cu/Ti codoping. The reversible phase transformation from P2 to O2 is obsd. As a representative cathode, Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2, the capacity contribution at a 4.2 V plateau has long been assigned to the redox of the Ni3+/Ni4+ couple, while at the same time it suffers large irreversible capacity loss during the initial discharging process. Our findings provide a new understanding of the chem. evolution in layered transition metal oxides at high operating voltage.  Increasing the covalency of the TM-O bond has been proven to be effective in suppressing the oxygen release and hence improving the electrochem. performance.  A new understanding of the oxygen evolution in layered transition metal oxides is proposed and the reductive coupling mechanism is employed to suppress the oxygen release through increasing the covalency of the TM-O bond.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref31" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b12204"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, J.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Z.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pang, W.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, K.-C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.-P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advanced P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>7/12</sub>Fe<sub>1/12</sub>O<sub>2</sub> Cathode Material with Suppressed P2âO2 Phase Transition Toward High-Performance Sodium-Ion battery</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Both n diffraction and NMR spectroscopy are used to elucidate the local structure, and they reveal that most of the Li ions are located in transition metal (TM) sites, preferably surrounded by Mn ions.  To characterize structural changes occurring upon electrochem. cycling, in situ synchrotron x-ray diffraction is conducted.  No significant phase transformation is obsd. up to 4.4 V charge for this material, unlike Li-free P2-type Na cathodes.  The presence of monovalent Li ions in the TM layers allows more Na ions to reside in the prismatic sites, stabilizing the overall charge balance of the compd.  Consequently, more Na ions remain in the compd. upon charge, the P2 structure is retained in the high voltage region, and the phase transformation is delayed.  Ex situ NMR is conducted on samples at different states of charge/discharge to track Li-ion site occupation changes.  Surprisingly, Li is mobile, some Li ions migrate from the TM layer to the Na layer at high voltage, and yet this process is highly reversible.  Novel design principles for Na cathode materials are proposed from an atomistic level understanding of the underlying electrochem. processes.  These principles enable one to devise an optimized, high capacity, and structurally stable compd. as a potential cathode material for high-energy Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1107/s0567739476001551"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shannon, R.                         cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unveiling the Role of Co in Improving the High-Rate Capability and Cycling Performance of Layered Na0.7Mn0.7Ni0.3-xCoxO2 Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Zheng-Yao; Zhang, Jicheng; Gao, Rui; Zhang, Heng; Hu, Zhongbo; Liu, Xiangfeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">24</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">15439-15448</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Co substitution has been extensively used to improve the electrochem. performances of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), but the role of Co has not been well understood.  Herein, we have comprehensively investigated the effects of Co substitution for Ni on the structure and electrochem. performances of Na0.7Mn0.7Ni0.3-xCoxO2 (x = 0, 0.1, 0.3) as cathode materials for SIBs.  In comparison with the Co-free sample, the high-rate capability and cycle performance have been greatly improved by the substitution of Co, and some new insights into the role of Co have been proposed for the first time.  With the substitution of Co3+ for Ni2+ the lattice parameter a decreases; however, c increases, and the d-spacing of the sodium-ion diffusion layer has been enlarged, which enhances the diffusion coeff. of the sodium ion and the high-rate capability of cathode materials.  In addn., Co substitution shortens the bond lengths of TM-O (TM = transition metal) and O-O due to the smaller size of Co3+ than Ni2+, which accounts for the decreased thickness and vol. of the TMO6 octahedron.  The contraction of TM-O and O-O bond lengths and the shrinkage of the TMO6 octahedron improve the structure stability and the cycle performance.  Last but not least, the aliovalent substitution of Co3+ for Ni2+ can improve the electronic cond. during the electrochem. reaction, which is also favorable to enhance the high-rate performance.  This study not only unveils the role of Co in improving the high-rate capability and the cycle stability of layered Na0.7Mn0.7Ni0.3-xCoxO2 cathode materials but also offers some new insights into designing high performance cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref36" class="refNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit36" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201803978"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.                         cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">P2-type Na2/3Mn1-xAlxO2 cathode material for sodium-ion batteries: Al-doped enhanced electrochemical properties and studies on the electrode kinetics</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pang, Wei-Lin; Zhang, Xiao-Hua; Guo, Jin-Zhi; Li, Jin-Yue; Yan, Xin; Hou, Bao-Hua; Guan, Hong-Yu; Wu, Xing-Long</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, Na-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as the promising alternative for Li-ion batteries.  Although layered P2-type transition metal oxides are an important class of cathode materials for SIBs, there are still some hurdles for the practical applications, including low specific capacity as well as poor cycling and rate properties.  Here, the electrochem. properties of layered Mn-based oxides were effectively improved via Al doping, which cannot only promote the formation of layered P2-type structure in the prepn. processes but also stabilize the lattice during the successive Na-intercalation/deintercalation due to suppression of the Jahn-Teller distortion of Mn3+.  Among the as-prepd. series of Na2/3Mn1-xAlxO2 (x =0, 1/18, 1/9, and 2/9), Na2/3Mn8/9Al1/9O2 with x =1/9 exhibits the optimal doping effect with the best electrochem. properties, in terms of the highest specific capacity of 162.3 mAh/g at 0.1 C, the highest rate capability, and the best cycling stability in comparison to the undoped Na2/3MnO2 and the other 2 materials with different Al-doped contents.  Both cyclic voltammetry at varied scan rates and galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique disclose the optimal electrode kinetics (the highest Na-diffusion coeff.) of the best Na2/3Mn8/9Al1/9O2.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref39" class="refNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit39" data-doi="10.1021/cm000721x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahn, J. Na+ batteries and battery-type desalinators.  P2 layered Na+ manganese-rich oxides have high theor. capacities and voltages for Na+ storage and are extensively investigated for non-aq. Na+ batteries.  However, the charge storage mechanism and factors controlling interlayer chem. and redox behavior of these materials in aq. electrolytes have not been detd.  Here, we take a significant step in establishing their aq. electrochem. behavior by investigating a series of P2 oxides that exhibit a range of stability in water and ambient air: Na0.62Ni0.22Mn0.66Fe0.10O2 (NaNMFe), Na0.61Ni0.22Mn0.66Co0.10O2 (NaNMCo), Na0.64Ni0.22Mn0.66Cu0.11O2 (NaNMCu), and Na0.64Mn0.62Cu0.31O2 (NaMCu).  Depending on the transition metal compn. and potential, all materials exhibit significant irreversible Na+ loss during the first anodic cycle followed by water intercalation into the interlayer.  The presence of water causes conversion into birnessite-like phases and microscopic exfoliation of the particles.  The interlayer affinity for water is primarily driven by the Na+ content, which can be tuned by the transition metal compn. and the max. anodic potential during electrochem. cycling. These results present the first studies on the structural effects of aq. electrochem. in P2 oxides, highlight the significant differences in the electrochem. behavior of P2 oxides in aq. vs. non-aq. electrolytes, and provide guidance on how to use the transition metal chem. to tune their aq. charge storage behavior.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b00097"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duffort, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Talaie, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Black, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nazar, L. The unprecedented insertion is demonstrated of carbonate ions in the vacancy-rich layered structure of P2-Na0.67[Mn0.5Fe0.5]O2 on exposure to CO2 and moisture, concomitant with oxidn. of Mn(III) to Mn(IV). Furthermore, it is shown that Ni-substituted materials P2-Na0.67[NixMn0.5+xFe0.5-2x]O2 are less prone to such reactivity and thus are more promising candidates for scalable processing.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Mn-rich layered oxides of P2 Na2/3Mn0.8Fe0.1Ti0.1O2 have been shown to exhibit a remarkably stable electrochem. performance even after exposure to moisture for extended periods of time.  Here, a detailed investigation of the electrochem. performance of pristine, protonated, and hydrated electrodes is reported.  Neutron powder diffraction and 23Na NMR are employed in order to correlate the overall electrochem. performance of each electrode with that of the as-synthesized crystal structure.  The effects of proton and water (or OH) moieties on the Na+ layers are discussed based on the electrochem. performance of each phase.
