        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries have garnered significant research attention in recent years due to the rising demand for large-scale energy storage solns. as well as the high abundance of sodium.  P2-type layered oxide materials have been identified as promising pos. electrode materials for sodium ion batteries.  Previously, P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 was shown to have a high operating voltage and high capacity but suffers from a step-like voltage curve and capacity loss during cycling, potentially due to its P2-O2 transition at high voltages.  One strategy to improve cycling performance has been to dope Ni2+ with other 2+ cations, such as Zn2+ or Mg2+, which improved capacity retention but significantly decreases reversible capacity, since these ions were not electrochem. active.  Since Cu2+ has been shown to be electrochem. active, we replaced Ni2+ with Cu2+, resulting in air-stable Na2/3Ni1/3-xCuxMn2/3O2 (0 â¤ x â¤ 1/3).  Both Ni2+/Ni4+ and Cu2+/Cu3+ participate in the redox reaction during cycling, capacity retention was greatly improved, and phase changes were suppressed during cycling without sacrificing much capacity.  The material retains a P2/OP4 structure even when cycled to high voltages.  The doping strategy is a promising approach for the future development of pos. electrode materials for sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref52" class="refNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit52" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201601292"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Asari, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoshida, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yaabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shiiba, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nakayama, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Understanding the Structural Evolution and Redox Mechanism of a NaFeO<sub>2</sub>âNaCoO<sub>2</sub> Solid Solution for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have become promising candidates for large-scale energy-storage systems because of the abundant Na resources and they have attracted considerable academic interest because of their unique behavior, such as their electrochem. activity for the Fe3+/Fe4+ redox couple.  The high-rate performance derived from the low Lewis-acidity of the Na+ ions is another advantage of Na-ion batteries and has been demonstrated in NaFe1/2Co1/2O2 solns.  Here, a solid soln. of NaFeO2-NaCoO2 is synthesized and the mechanisms behind their excellent electrochem. performance are studied in comparison to those of their resp. end-members.  The combined anal. of operando X-ray diffraction, ex situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy, and d. functional theory (DFT) calcns. for Na1-xFe1/2Co1/2O2 reveals that the O3-type phase transforms into a P3-type phase coupled with Na+/vacancy ordering, which has not been obsd. in O3-type NaFeO2.  The substitution of Co for Fe stabilizes the P3-type phase formed by sodium extn. and could suppress the irreversible structural change that is usually obsd. in O3-type NaFeO2, resulting in a better cycle retention and higher rate performance.  Although no ordering of the transition metal ions is seen in the neutron diffraction expts., as supported by Monte-Carlo simulations, the formation of a superlattice originating from the Na+/vacancy ordering is found by synchrotron X-ray diffraction for Na0.5Fe1/2Co1/2O2, which may involve a potential step in the charge/discharge profiles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref53" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1149/2.0151514jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">ReviewâPractical Issues and Future Perspective for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Layered Lepidocrocite Type Structure Isolated by Revisiting the Sol-Gel Chemistry of Anatase TiO2: A New Anode Material for Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ma, Jiwei; Reeves, Kyle G.; Porras Gutierrez, Ana-Gabriela; Body, Monique; Legein, Christophe; Kakinuma, Katsuyoshi; Borkiewicz, Olaf J.; Chapman, Karena W.; Groult, Henri; Salanne, Mathieu; Dambournet, Damien</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Searches for new electrode materials for batteries must take into account financial and environmental costs to be useful in practical devices.  The sol-gel chem. has been widely used to design and implement new concepts for the emergence of advanced materials such as hydride org.-inorg. composites.  Here, we show that the simple reaction system including titanium alkoxide and water can be used to stabilize a new class of electrode materials.  By investigating the crystn. path of anatase TiO2, an X-ray amorphous intermediate phase has been identified whose local structure probed by the pair distribution function, 1H solid-state NMR and d. functional theory (DFT) calcns., consists of a layered type structure as found in the lepidocrocite.  This phase presents the following general formula Ti2-x.box.xO4-4x(OH)4xÂ·nH2O (x â¼ 0.5) where the substitution of oxide by hydroxide anions leads to the formation of titanium vacancies (.box.) and H2O mols. are located in interlayers.  Solid-state 1H NMR has enabled us to characterize three main hydroxide environments, Ti.box.-OH, Ti2.box.2-OH, and Ti3.box.-OH, and layered H2O mols.  The electrochem. properties of this phase were investigated vs lithium and were shown to be very promising with reversible capacities of around 200 mAhÂ·g-1 and an operating voltage of 1.55 V.  We further showed that the lithium intercalation proceeds via a solid-soln. mechanism. 7Li solid-state NMR and DFT calcns. allowed us to identify lithium host sites that are located at the titanium vacancies and interlayer space with lithium being solvated by structural water mols.  The easy fabrication, the absence of lithium, easier recycling, and the encouraging properties make this class of materials very attractive for competitive electrodes for batteries.  We thus demonstrate that revisiting an "old" chem. with advanced characterization tools allows one to discover new materials of technol. relevance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref58" class="refNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit58" data-doi="10.1039/c6ta09601h"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">ClÃ©ment, R. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Middlemiss, D. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alvarado, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Y. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grey, C. P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Direct Evidence for High Na<sup>+</sup> Mobility and High Voltage Structural Processes in P2-Na<sub>x</sub>[Li<sub>y</sub>Ni<sub>z</sub>Mn<sub>1âyâz</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (x, y, z â¤ 1) Cathodes from Solid-State NMR and DFT Calculations</span>. <i>J. Mater. Chem. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">4129</span>â <span class="NLM_lpage">4143</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c6ta09601h</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D4129-4143%26journal%3DJ.%2BMater.%2BChem.%2BA%26author%3DR.%2BJ.%2BCl%25C3%25A9ment%26author%3DJ.%2BXu%26author%3DD.%2BS.%2BMiddlemiss%26author%3DJ.%2BAlvarado%26author%3DC.%2BMa%26author%3DY.%2BS.%2BMeng%26author%3DC.%2BP.%2BGrey%26title%3DDirect%2BEvidence%2Bfor%2BHigh%2BNa%252B%2BMobility%2Band%2BHigh%2BVoltage%2BStructural%2BProcesses%2Bin%2BP2-Nax%255BLiyNizMn1%25E2%2580%2593y%25E2%2580%2593z%255DO2%2B%2528x%252C%2By%252C%2Bz%2B%25E2%2589%25A4%2B1%2529%2BCathodes%2Bfrom%2BSolid-State%2BNMR%2Band%2BDFT%2BCalculations%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc6ta09601h&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c00386&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc6ta09601h&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit58R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">58</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Direct evidence for high Na+ mobility and high voltage structural processes in P2-Nax[LiyNizMn1-y-z]O2 (x, y, z â¤ 1) cathodes from solid-state NMR and DFT calculations</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Clement, R. J.; Xu, J.; Middlemiss, D. S.; Alvarado, J.; Ma, C.; Meng, Y. S.; Grey, C. P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4129-4143</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Structural processes occurring upon electrochem. cycling in P2-Nax[LiyNizMn1-y-z]O2 (x, y, z â¤ 1) cathode materials are investigated using 23Na and 7Li solid-state NMR (ssNMR).  The interpretation of the complex paramagnetic NMR data obtained for various electrochem.-cycled NaxNi1/3Mn2/3O2 and NaxLi0.12Ni0.22Mn0.66O2 samples is assisted by state-of-the-art hybrid Hartree-Fock/d. functional theory calcns.  Two Na crystallog. environments are present in P2-Nax[LiyNizMn1-y-z]O2 compds., yet a single 23Na NMR signal is obsd. with a shift in-between those computed for edge- and face-centered prismatic sites, indicating that Na-ion motion between sites in the P2 layers results in an av. signal.  This is the first time that exptl. and theor. evidence are provided for fast Na-ion motion (on the timescale of the NMR expts.) in the interlayer space in P2-type NaxTMO2 materials.  A full assignment of the 7Li NMR data confirms that Li substitution delays the P2 to O2 phase transformation taking place in NaxNi1/3Mn2/3O2 over the range 1/3 â¥ xNa â¥ 0. 23Na ssNMR data demonstrate that NaxNi1/3Mn2/3O2 samples charged to â¥3.7 V are extremely moisture sensitive once they are removed from the cell, water mols. being readily intercalated within the P2 layers leading to an addnl. Na signal between 400 and 250 ppm.  By contrast, the lithiated material NaxLi0.12Ni0.22Mn0.66O2 shows no sign of hydration until it is charged to â¥4.4 V.  Since both TMO2 layer glides and water intercalation become increasingly favorable as more vacancies are present in the Na layers, the higher stability of the Li-doped P2 phase at high voltage can be accounted for by its higher Na content at all stages of cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript"                 10.1038/natrevmats.2016.13</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Promise and reality of post-lithium-ion batteries with high energy densities</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Choi, Jang Wook; Aurbach, Doron</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Reviews Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">16013</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy d. is the main property of rechargeable batteries that has driven the entire technol. forward in past decades.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) now surpass other, previously competitive battery types (for example, lead-acid and nickel metal hydride) but still require extensive further improvement to, in particular, extend the operation hours of mobile IT devices and the driving mileages of all-elec. vehicles.  In this Review, we present a crit. overview of a wide range of post-LIB materials and systems that could have a pivotal role in meeting such demands.  We divide battery systems into two categories: near-term and long-term technologies.  To provide a realistic and balanced perspective, we describe the operating principles and remaining issues of each post-LIB technol., and also evaluate these materials under com. cell configurations.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1038/s41560-018-0107-2"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schmuch, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wagner, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">HÃ¶rpel, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Placke, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Winter, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Performance and Cost of Materials for Lithium-Based Rechargeable Automotive Batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                 10.1038/s41560-018-0107-2</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Performance and cost of materials for lithium-based rechargeable automotive batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Schmuch, Richard; Wagner, Ralf; Hoerpel, Gerhard; Placke, Tobias; Winter, Martin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">It is widely accepted that for elec. vehicles to be accepted by consumers and to achieve wide market penetration, ranges of at least 500 km at an affordable cost are required.  Therefore, significant improvements to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in terms of energy d. and cost along the battery value chain are required, while other key performance indicators, such as lifetime, safety, fast-charging ability and low-temp. performance, need to be enhanced or at least sustained.  Here, we review advances and challenges in LIB materials for automotive applications, in particular with respect to cost and performance parameters.  The prodn. processes of anode and cathode materials are discussed, focusing on material abundance and cost.  Advantages and challenges of different types of electrolyte for automotive batteries are examd.  Finally, energy densities and costs of promising battery chemistries are critically evaluated along with an assessment of the potential to fulfil the ambitious targets of elec. vehicle propulsion.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1038/natrevmats.2018.13"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vaalma, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weil, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Cost and Resource Analysis of Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nat.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered P2 oxides are promising cathode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries.  In this work, we systematically investigate the effects of transition-metal (TM) mixing on Na ordering and kinetics in the NaxCo1-yMnyO2 model system using d.-functional-theory (DFT) calcns.  The DFT-predicted 0-K stability diagrams indicate that Co-Mn mixing reduces the energetic differences between Na orderings, which may account for the redn. of the no. of phase transformations obsd. during the cycling of mixed-TM P2 layered oxides compared to a single TM.  Using ab initio mol.-dynamics simulations and nudged elastic-band calcns., we show that the TM compn. at the Na(1) (face-sharing) site has a strong influence on the Na site energies, which in turn impacts the kinetics of Na diffusion towards the end of the charge.  By employing a site-percolation model, we establish theor. upper and lower bounds for TM concns. based on their effect on Na(1) site energies, providing a framework to rationally tune mixed-TM compns. for optimal Na diffusion.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.3389/fchem.2019.00335"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Air-Stable Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> Cathodes for Sodium Storage</span>. <i>Front. Sodium layered transition metal oxides (NaxTMO2) are proper Na-ion cathode materials because of low cost and high theor. capacity.  Currently most researchers focus on the improvement of electrochem. performance such as high rate capability and long cycling stability.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered O3-type sodium oxides (NaMO2, M=transition metal) commonly exhibit an O3-P3 phase transition, which occurs at a low redox voltage of â¼3 V (vs. Na+/Na) during sodium extn. and insertion, with the result that almost 50 % of their total capacity lies at this low voltage region, and they possess insufficient energy d. as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (NIBs).  Therefore, development of high-voltage O3-type cathodes remains challenging because it is difficult to raise the phase-transition voltage by reasonable structure modulation.  A new example of O3-type sodium insertion materials is presented for use in NIBs.  The designed O3-type Na0.7Ni0.35Sn0.65O2 material displays a highest redox potential of 3.7 V (vs. Na+/Na) among the reported O3-type materials based on the Ni2+/Ni3+ couple, by virtue of its increased Ni-O bond ionicity through reduced orbital overlap between transition metals and oxygen within the MO2 slabs.  This study provides an orbital-level understanding of the operating potentials of the nominal redox couples for O3-NaMO2 cathodes.  The strategy described could be used to tailor electrodes for improved performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref22" class="rightTabRefNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit22" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.7b05176"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, H.-R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">OuYang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stavitski, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wan, L.-J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Designing Air-Stable O3-Type Cathode Materials by Combined Structure Modulation for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         rightTab-cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Revisiting the Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 Cathode: Oxygen Redox Chemistry and Oxygen Release Suppression</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Yi; Wu, Miaomiao; Ma, Jiwei; Wei, Guangfeng; Ling, Yun; Zhang, Renyuan; Huang, Yunhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Central Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium layered transition metal oxides have been considered as promising cathode materials for sodium ion batteries due to their large capacity and high operating voltage.  However, mechanism investigations of chem. evolution and capacity failure at high voltage are inadequate.  As a representative cathode, Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2, the capacity contribution at a 4.2 V plateau has long been assigned to the redox of the Ni3+/Ni4+ couple, while at the same time it suffers large irreversible capacity loss during the initial discharging process.  In this work, we prove that the capacity at the 4.2 V plateau is contributed to the irreversible O2-/O2n-/O2 evolution based on in situ differential electrochem. mass spectrometry and d. functional theory calcn. results.  Besides, a phenomenon of oxygen release and subsequent surface lattice densification is obsd., which is responsible for the large irreversible capacity loss during the initial cycle.  Furthermore, the oxygen release is successfully suppressed by Fe substitution due to the formation of a unique Fe-(O-O) species, which effectively stabilizes the reversibility of the O2-/O2n- redox at high operating voltage.  Our findings provide a new understanding of the chem. evolution in layered transition metal oxides at high operating voltage.  Increasing the covalency of the TM-O bond has been proven to be effective in suppressing the oxygen release and hence improving the electrochem. performance.  A new understanding of the oxygen evolution in layered transition metal oxides is proposed and the reductive coupling mechanism is employed to suppress the oxygen release through increasing the covalency of the TM-O bond.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref31" class="rightTabRefNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit31" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b12204"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, J.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Z.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pang, W.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, K.-C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.-P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advanced P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>7/12</sub>Fe<sub>1/12</sub>O<sub>2</sub> Cathode Material with Suppressed P2âO2 Phase Transition Toward High-Performance Sodium-Ion battery</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-substituted layered P2-Na0.80[Li0.12Ni0.22Mn0.66]O2 is studied as an advanced cathode material for Na-ion batteries.  Both n diffraction and NMR spectroscopy are used to elucidate the local structure, and they reveal that most of the Li ions are located in transition metal (TM) sites, preferably surrounded by Mn ions.  To characterize structural changes occurring upon electrochem. cycling, in situ synchrotron x-ray diffraction is conducted.  No significant phase transformation is obsd. up to 4.4 V charge for this material, unlike Li-free P2-type Na cathodes.  The presence of monovalent Li ions in the TM layers allows more Na ions to reside in the prismatic sites, stabilizing the overall charge balance of the compd.  Consequently, more Na ions remain in the compd. upon charge, the P2 structure is retained in the high voltage region, and the phase transformation is delayed.  Ex situ NMR is conducted on samples at different states of charge/discharge to track Li-ion site occupation changes.  Surprisingly, Li is mobile, some Li ions migrate from the TM layer to the Na layer at high voltage, and yet this process is highly reversible.  Novel design principles for Na cathode materials are proposed from an atomistic level understanding of the underlying electrochem. processes.  These principles enable one to devise an optimized, high capacity, and structurally stable compd. as a potential cathode material for high-energy Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref34" class="rightTabRefNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit34" data-doi="10.1107/s0567739476001551"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shannon, R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Co substitution has been extensively used to improve the electrochem. performances of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), but the role of Co has not been well understood.  Herein, we have comprehensively investigated the effects of Co substitution for Ni on the structure and electrochem. performances of Na0.7Mn0.7Ni0.3-xCoxO2 (x = 0, 0.1, 0.3) as cathode materials for SIBs.  In comparison with the Co-free sample, the high-rate capability and cycle performance have been greatly improved by the substitution of Co, and some new insights into the role of Co have been proposed for the first time.  With the substitution of Co3+ for Ni2+ the lattice parameter a decreases; however, c increases, and the d-spacing of the sodium-ion diffusion layer has been enlarged, which enhances the diffusion coeff. of the sodium ion and the high-rate capability of cathode materials.  In addn., Co substitution shortens the bond lengths of TM-O (TM = transition metal) and O-O due to the smaller size of Co3+ than Ni2+, which accounts for the decreased thickness and vol. of the TMO6 octahedron.  The contraction of TM-O and O-O bond lengths and the shrinkage of the TMO6 octahedron improve the structure stability and the cycle performance.  Last but not least, the aliovalent substitution of Co3+ for Ni2+ can improve the electronic cond. during the electrochem. reaction, which is also favorable to enhance the high-rate performance.  This study not only unveils the role of Co in improving the high-rate capability and the cycle stability of layered Na0.7Mn0.7Ni0.3-xCoxO2 cathode materials but also offers some new insights into designing high performance cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref36" class="rightTabRefNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit36" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201803978"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y. J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lithium MAS NMR Studies of Cathode Materials for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Solid State Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2003</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">883</span>â <span class="NLM_lpage">894</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/s1293-2558(03)00113-4</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium MAS NMR studies of cathode materials for lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Grey, Clare P.; Lee, Young Joo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Solid State Sciences</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2003</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">883-894</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SSSCFJ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1293-2558</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier SAS</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The application of NMR spectroscopy to study the cathode materials used in lithium-ion batteries is described.  The research described in this review article clearly demonstrates that it is now possible to obtain a wealth of information from the spectra of these materials, even in the paramagnetic or metallic state, both prior and following electrochem. cycling of a battery.  First, we review the work performed at Stony Brook and elsewhere, initially to develop a detailed understanding of the shift mechanisms that result in the large shifts in the spectra of paramagnetic lithium manganates, and then, to apply this knowledge to probe local structure and electronic properties in a range of materials.  NMR of a wider range of cathode materials is then briefly discussed.  Finally, the exptl. methods typically used in this field are briefly described.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref55" class="rightTabRefNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit55" data-doi="10.1103/physrevb.77.075407"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grillo, M. E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Finnis, M. W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ranke, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Surface Structure and Water Adsorption on Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (111): Spin-Density Functional Theory and on-site Coulomb Interactions</span>. <i>Phys.                 10.1103/physrevb.77.075407</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Surface structure and water adsorption on Fe3O4(111). Spin-density functional theory and on-site Coulomb interactions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Grillo, M. E.; Finnis, M. W.; Ranke, W.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review B: Condensed Matter and Materials Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2008</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">77</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">075407/1-075407/5</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PRBMDO</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1098-0121</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Physical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The surface structure of magnetite Fe3O4(111) in contact with O and water is investigated using spin-d. functional theory plus on-site Coulomb interactions.  The present results unravel apparent contradictions in the exptl. data regarding the equil. stoichiometry of the bare surface termination.  Both for 298 and 1200 K, the equil. structure is terminated by (1)/(4) monolayer (ML) of Fe on top of a full O layer, consistent with an earlier LEED anal.  Nonetheless, the calcd. neg. slope of the surface energies vs O partial pressure shows that a (1)/(2) ML Fe termination would become stable under O-poor conditions at high temps., in agreement to interpretation of scanning tunneling microscopy expts.  Initial water adsorption is dissociative and sats. when all Fe sites are occupied by OH groups, while the H atoms bind to surface oxygen.  Further, water bridges the OH and H groups resulting in a unique type of H-bonded mol. water with its O forming a hydronium-ion-like structure OH3+-OH.  This water structure is different from the water dimeric structures found as yet on oxide and metal surfaces for partially dissocd. (H2O-OH-H) overlayers.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref56" class="rightTabRefNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit56" data-doi="10.1021/jp109264b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zasada, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Piskorz, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cristol, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Paul, J.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kotarba, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sojka, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Periodic Density Functional Theory and Atomistic Thermodynamic Studies of Cobalt Spinel Nanocrystals in Wet Environment: Molecular Interpretation of Water Adsorption Equilibria</span>. <i>J. Phys. Chem. C</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2010</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>114</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">22245</span>â <span class="NLM_lpage">22253</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/jp109264b</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Periodic density functional theory and atomistic thermodynamic studies of cobalt spinel nanocrystals in wet environment: molecular interpretation of water adsorption equilibria</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zasada, Filip; Piskorz, Witold; Cristol, Sylvain; Paul, Jean-Francois; Kotarba, Andrzej; Sojka, Zbigniew</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">114</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">50</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this paper we present a theor. study of water sorption on cobalt spinel nanocrystals by means of plane-wave periodic d. functional theory (DFT) calcns. jointly with statistical thermodn.  The three most stable (100), (110), and (111) planes exposed by Co3O4 were considered, and their stabilization upon water adsorption is discussed in detail.  The calcd. changes in free enthalpy of the investigated system under different hydration conditions along with the Wulff construction were used to predict the rhombicuboctahedral equil. morphol. of cobalt spinel nanocrystals in different conditions, which corresponds very well to the exptl. transmission electron microscopic (TEM) images.  Two-dimensional surface coverage vs. temp. and pressure diagrams were constructed for each of the examd. (100), (110), and (111) planes to illustrate water adsorption processes in a concise way.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref57"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref57" class="rightTabRefNumLink">57</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit57" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.7b02674"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Reeves, K. G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Porras
Gutierrez, A.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Body, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Legein, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kakinuma, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Borkiewicz, O. W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Groult, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Salanne, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dambournet, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Layered Lepidocrocite Type Structure Isolated by Revisiting the SolâGel Chemistry of Anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>: A New Anode Material for Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>29</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">8313</span>â <span class="NLM_lpage">8324</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.chemmater.7b02674</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Layered Lepidocrocite Type Structure Isolated by Revisiting the Sol-Gel Chemistry of Anatase TiO2: A New Anode Material for Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ma, Jiwei; Reeves, Kyle G.; Porras Gutierrez, Ana-Gabriela; Body, Monique; Legein, Christophe; Kakinuma, Katsuyoshi; Borkiewicz, Olaf J.; Chapman, Karena W.; Groult, Henri; Salanne, Mathieu; Dambournet, Damien</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">29</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">19</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">8313-8324</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Searches for new electrode materials for batteries must take into account financial and environmental costs to be useful in practical devices.  The sol-gel chem. has been widely used to design and implement new concepts for the emergence of advanced materials such as hydride org.-inorg. composites.  Here, we show that the simple reaction system including titanium alkoxide and water can be used to stabilize a new class of electrode materials.  By investigating the crystn. path of anatase TiO2, an X-ray amorphous intermediate phase has been identified whose local structure probed by the pair distribution function, 1H solid-state NMR and d. functional theory (DFT) calcns., consists of a layered type structure as found in the lepidocrocite.  This phase presents the following general formula Ti2-x.box.xO4-4x(OH)4xÂ·nH2O (x â¼ 0.5) where the substitution of oxide by hydroxide anions leads to the formation of titanium vacancies (.box.) and H2O mols. are located in interlayers.  Solid-state 1H NMR has enabled us to characterize three main hydroxide environments, Ti.box.-OH, Ti2.box.2-OH, and Ti3.box.-OH, and layered H2O mols.  The electrochem. properties of this phase were investigated vs lithium and were shown to be very promising with reversible capacities of around 200 mAhÂ·g-1 and an operating voltage of 1.55 V.  We further showed that the lithium intercalation proceeds via a solid-soln. mechanism. 7Li solid-state NMR and DFT calcns. allowed us to identify lithium host sites that are located at the titanium vacancies and interlayer space with lithium being solvated by structural water mols.  The easy fabrication, the absence of lithium, easier recycling, and the encouraging properties make this class of materials very attractive for competitive electrodes for batteries.  We thus demonstrate that revisiting an "old" chem. with advanced characterization tools allows one to discover new materials of technol. relevance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref58" class="rightTabRefNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit58" data-doi="10.1039/c6ta09601h"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">ClÃ©ment, R. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Middlemiss, D. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alvarado, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Y. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grey, C. P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Direct Evidence for High Na<sup>+</sup> Mobility and High Voltage Structural Processes in P2-Na<sub>x</sub>[Li<sub>y</sub>Ni<sub>z</sub>Mn<sub>1âyâz</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (x, y, z â¤ 1) Cathodes from Solid-State NMR and DFT Calculations</span>. <i>J. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">4129</span>â <span class="NLM_lpage">4143</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c6ta09601h</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit58R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">58</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Direct evidence for high Na+ mobility and high voltage structural processes in P2-Nax[LiyNizMn1-y-z]O2 (x, y, z â¤ 1) cathodes from solid-state NMR and DFT calculations</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Clement, R. J.; Xu, J.; Middlemiss, D. S.; Alvarado, J.; Ma, C.; Meng, Y. S.; Grey, C. P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4129-4143</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Structural processes occurring upon electrochem. cycling in P2-Nax[LiyNizMn1-y-z]O2 (x, y, z â¤ 1) cathode materials are investigated using 23Na and 7Li solid-state NMR (ssNMR).  The interpretation of the complex paramagnetic NMR data obtained for various electrochem.-cycled NaxNi1/3Mn2/3O2 and NaxLi0.12Ni0.22Mn0.66O2 samples is assisted by state-of-the-art hybrid Hartree-Fock/d. functional theory calcns.  Two Na crystallog. environments are present in P2-Nax[LiyNizMn1-y-z]O2 compds., yet a single 23Na NMR signal is obsd. with a shift in-between those computed for edge- and face-centered prismatic sites, indicating that Na-ion motion between sites in the P2 layers results in an av. signal.  This is the first time that exptl. and theor. evidence are provided for fast Na-ion motion (on the timescale of the NMR expts.) in the interlayer space in P2-type NaxTMO2 materials.  A full assignment of the 7Li NMR data confirms that Li substitution delays the P2 to O2 phase transformation taking place in NaxNi1/3Mn2/3O2 over the range 1/3 â¥ xNa â¥ 0. 23Na ssNMR data demonstrate that NaxNi1/3Mn2/3O2 samples charged to â¥3.7 V are extremely moisture sensitive once they are removed from the cell, water mols. being readily intercalated within the P2 layers leading to an addnl. Na signal between 400 and 250 ppm.  By contrast, the lithiated material NaxLi0.12Ni0.22Mn0.66O2 shows no sign of hydration until it is charged to â¥4.4 V.  Since both TMO2 layer glides and water intercalation become increasingly favorable as more vacancies are present in the Na layers, the higher stability of the Li-doped P2 phase at high voltage can be accounted for by its higher Na content at all stages of cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i18">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c00386?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c00386</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">EDS, HRTEM images, <sup>7</sup>Li solid-state NMR spectrum, and other characterization data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/suppl_file/am0c00386_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c00386/suppl_file/am0c00386_si_001.pdf">am0c00386_si_001.pdf (1.53 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

        <script nonce="93187fd73897075b-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="93187fd73897075b-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")

        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());

        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

        <script nonce="93187fd73897075b-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
